1 
ya should be long. 
2 
The short syllable with which the line opens is metrically too many. 
3 
In Kern 1900 these variant readings are erroneously listed under stanza 97. 
4 
Kern 1900 skips five numbers and jumps from 92 to 98 instead of going on to 93. His 
references to the stanzas in the critical apparatus make the same jump (but remarkably here 
the jump is made after stanza 93). Poerbatjaraka (2010) applies the correct numbering with 
simultaneous reference to Kernâ€™s numbering; I have done the same. The Old Javanese-
English dictionary by Zoetmulder retains Kernâ€™s numbering.

APPENDICES



APPENDIX 1 
Unknown metres in the R?m?ya?a 
 
 
 
 
Poerbatjaraka concluded his famous article of 1932 on the Old Javanese 
R?m?ya?a with an overview of all the metres used in the text, ordered (1) by 
sarga, and (2) by name (Poerbatjaraka 1932:201-214; the overview is 
reproduced in Poerbatjaraka 2010). Among the 79 metres1 there are ten 
metres without a name. They are not mentioned in the handbooks on 
prosody, either Indian or Indonesian. Poerbatjaraka called them â€˜Onbekendâ€™ 
(â€˜Unknownâ€™), while numbering them 1-10 in the order in which he found 
them in the text. The first three columns of Table 1 list these unknown 
metres.  
About twenty years later Hooykaas was able to reduce Poerbatjarakaâ€™s 
unknown metres from ten to six. In fact, there had only been nine unknown 
metres, as numbers 4 and 7 were identical, but Hooykaas must have 
overlooked this as he moved only number 7 to the category of known metres 
(Hooykaas 1955: Appendix I, stanzas 9.89-91 and 22.13-40; in his table of 
â€˜unknown metresâ€™ on page 54 number 7 is counted as unknown). The fourth 
column of Table 1 lists Hooykaasâ€™s amendments.

Another twenty years later Zoetmulder shortened Poerbatjarakaâ€™s list 
slightly further by identifying another metre (by a name known in Bali but 
not in the Indian handbooks). At the same time, however, he increased the 
number of unknown metres by rejecting one of Hooykaasâ€™s identifications 
and by redirecting five of Poerbatjarakaâ€™s known metres to the category of 
unknown ones. One metre was even split into two unknown metres 
(Zoetmulder 1974:451-472; note that Poerbatjaraka and Zoetmulder in the 
case of known metres do not always agree on their identity and 
distribution). The fifth column of Table 1 shows what had become in 1974 of 
Poerbatjarakaâ€™s ten unknown metres as listed in 1932. Table 2 shows which 
of Poerbatjarakaâ€™s known metres were relabelled by Zoetmulder. 
Although Zoetmulder does not explain his relabelling, it is clear why he 
relabelled stanzas 4.72 and 21.1-13, which according to Poerbatjaraka are 
written in Aupacchandasika. This metre occurs in many places but not here. 
Poerbatjarakaâ€™s commentary on 21.1-13, that we are dealing with an 
Aupacchandasika where something is missing, is unsatisfactory.1 What we 
do find in 4.72 and 21.1-13 are two different and as yet unknown metres. 
The metre of stanza 15.47 also raises a difference of opinion but in this case 
there seems room for discussion. Poerbatjaraka notes a Drutawilambita, 
without further comment. Lines 3 and 4, however, each contain one short 
syllable too many, enough for Zoetmulder to decide on a different metre, be 
it still unknown which one. He also found that there are no other places 
using this metre in the Old Javanese R?m?ya?a or in any Old Javanese text 
scrutinized by him. One might add that a change of metre within a stanza â€“ 
the phenomenon we are facing here â€“ does occur in the Old Javanese 
R?m?ya?a but is restricted to two sets of metres, Indrabajra-Upendrabajra 
                                                
1 
â€˜De even-vierels met steeds Ã©Ã©n laatste lettergreep te kort, behalve strophe 7 en 13â€™ (â€˜The 
even quarters always one final syllable short, except stanzas 7 and 13â€™). There are more 
differences than just the missing of one syllable. Poerbatjaraka did not comment on stanza 
4.72. He added a question mark in the case of 21.1-13, showing that he himself was also not 
certain about identifying the metre as Aupacchandasika.

621 
and Wang?astha-Indrawang?a; the many stanzas involved form a parade of 
all sorts of possible combinations. The Drutawilambita, on the other hand, 
although it is a common metre in Old Javanese texts, is found nowhere else 
except here in combination with another metre within a stanza. The uncom-
mon nature and low frequency of this particular combination call for a closer 
inspection of the text of stanza 15.47. For the time being I have retained the 
name Drutawilambita, with a note saying that something seems to be 
wrong. 
Zoetmulderâ€™s reason for relabelling is less clear in the three remaining 
cases. The Mr
g?ng?a, a common metre in Old Javanese texts and known by 
several names, occurs in the Old Javanese R?m?ya?a only once, in 22.58-66. 
Zoetmulderâ€™s listing of the metre used there as unidentified in his overview 
of metres, instead of under Mr
g?ng?a further down the same page, must be 
a mistake (see Zoetmulder 1974:462, entries XIX 3 and 6). I have retained the 
name Mr
g?ng?a. 
Contrary to the Mr
g?ng?a, the Rambh? is infrequent. In the entire Old 
Javanese literature â€“ going by Zoetmulder â€“ it occurs only in stanzas 25.66-
67 of the R?m?ya?a. Poerbatjaraka borrowed the name from Colebrookeâ€™s 
1808 study on Sanskrit metre (see Poerbatjaraka 1932:211). In view of the 
prosodic pattern there can be no doubt that he and Zoetmulder are talking 
about the same metre. In Poerbatjarakaâ€™s article there is a slight misprint:   
        instead of          . Could this have led 
Zoetmulder to misread the pattern and take it for a different metre? I have 
kept the name Rambh?. 
The same can be said about the Aparawaktra. It does not occur in other 
Old Javanese texts. In the R?m?ya?a it is common, while its name is 
mentioned in the handbooks on Indian prosody, where Poerbatjaraka took it 
from. I cannot think of a reason why Zoetmulder would drop the name and 
regard it as an unknown metre. The name Aparawaktra therefore has also 
been retained.

Appendix 1 
Finally, a problem similar to that of stanza 15.47 arises for stanzas 3.53-54. 
On the one hand, small but persistent prosodic differences point to two 
different metres (Unknown 13 and 14), while on the other hand a once-only 
combination of two metres in one stanza and, moreover, a rather puzzling 
distribution over two stanzas suggest that the text is out of order here. I note 
in passing that the metre I have labelled Unknown 13 is indeed mentioned 
by Zoetmulder, though not in the present context: he refers to occurrences of 
this metre in other texts, not in the R?m?ya?a (Zoetmulder 1974:459, entry 
XV 6). My findings are summarized in Table 3.
